{"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01GxTczFewjA1ef9Up2WMw3A","type":"tool_result","content":"{\"content\":\"1: # apiserver Architecture\\n2: \\n3: ## 1. Server Composition\\n4: \\n5: The `kube-apiserver` binary is not one server, but a **server chain** of three distinct\\n6: `GenericAPIServer` instances: the core Kubernetes API, API extensions (CRDs), and the aggregation\\n7: layer.\\n8: \\n9: This composition is managed by a layered configuration system, starting with command-line\\n10: flags parsed by `options` structs (e.g., `RecommendedOptions` in `pkg/server/options`), which\\n11: populate a `Config` object that is then used to instantiate the `GenericAPIServer`\\n12: instances. The construction of this delegation chain can be found in the `CreateServerChain`\\n13: function in `cmd/kube-apiserver/app/server.go`.\\n14: \\n15: ```mermaid\\n16: graph TD\\n17:     subgraph Incoming Request\\n18:         direction LR\\n19:         A[User/Client] --> B{/apis/apps/v1/deployments};\\n20:     end\\n21: \\n22:     subgraph kube-apiserver process\\n23:         direction LR\\n24:         B --> C[Aggregator Server];\\n25:         C -- Not an APIService --> D[Kube API Server];\\n26:         D -- Handles Request --> E[REST Storage];\\n27:         C -- Is an APIService --> F[Proxy to Extension API Server];\\n28:         D -- Not a Core API --> G[API Extensions Server];\\n29:         G -- Handles CRD --> E;\\n30:     end\\n31: ```\\n32: \\n33: 1.  **Aggregator Server (`kube-aggregator`):**\\n34:     *   **Purpose:** Handles the `apiregistration.k8s.io` API and acts as a reverse proxy for\\n35:         extension API servers. This functionality was designed to allow third-party APIs to be\\n36:         \\\"aggregated\\\" into the main Kubernetes API server seamlessly.\\n37:     *   **Mechanism:** It watches `APIService` objects. When a request arrives (e.g., for\\n38:         `/apis/mycompany.com/v1/myresources`), it checks if an `APIService` has \\\"claimed\\\"\\n39:         that path. If so, it uses a `ServiceResolver` to find the IP of the backing\\n40:         `Service` and proxies the request.\\n41:     *   **Use Case:** This pattern is for programmatic, high-control extensions that require\\n42:         custom business logic (e.g., non-CRUD subresources like `/logs`) or alternative\\n43:         storage backends.\\n44:     *   **Delegation:** If no `APIService` matches, it delegates the request to the next\\n45:         server in the chain.\\n46: \\n47: 2.  **Kube API Server (Core):**\\n48:     *   **Purpose:** Serves all the built-in Kubernetes APIs (`core/v1`, `apps/v1`, etc.).\\n49:     *   **Mechanism:** This is the main server, configured with all the core REST storage\\n50:         strategies.\\n51:     *   **Delegation:** If a request is for a path that is not a core API (e.g., for a CRD),\\n52:         it delegates the request to the next server in the chain.\\n53: \\n54: 3.  **API Extensions Server (`apiextensions-apiserver`):**\\n55:     *   **Purpose:** Handles the `apiextensions.k8s.io` API, which manages\\n56:         `CustomResourceDefinition` objects (CRDs). The evolution of CRDs from a simple extension\\n57:         mechanism to a feature-rich system with validation, versioning, and defaulting is\\n58:         documented in a series of KEPs, starting with the graduation to GA in Kubernetes v1.16.\\n59:     *   **Mechanism:** When a CRD is created, this server dynamically creates and installs a\\n60:         new REST storage handler for the new resource, making it immediately available.\\n61:     *   **Use Case:** CRDs are the most common extension pattern, offering a declarative,\\n62:         schema-based way to define new resource types that are stored in etcd and require no\\n63:         custom API server code.\\n64:     *   **Delegation:** It is the end of the chain. If it cannot handle a request, a `404 Not\\n65:         Found` is returned.\\n66: \\n67: ## 2. Handler Chain\\n68: \\n69: Every request flows through a standard chain of HTTP handlers (filters). The request body\\n70: is not deserialized until it has passed authentication and authorization. The default handler\\n71: chain is constructed by the `DefaultBuildHandlerChain` function in\\n72: `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/config.go`.\\n73: \\n74: ```mermaid\\n75: sequenceDiagram\\n76:     participant Client\\n77:     participant Handler Chain\\n78:     participant Authentication\\n79:     participant Authorization\\n80:     participant Priority and Fairness\\n81:     participant Admission Control\\n82:     participant REST Endpoint\\n83: \\n84:     Client->>Handler Chain: Request\\n85:     Handler Chain->>Authentication: Authenticate\\n86:     Authentication-->>Handler Chain: User Info\\n87:     Handler Chain->>Authorization: Authorize\\n88:     Authorization-->>Handler Chain: Allowed/Denied\\n89:     Handler Chain->>Priority and Fairness: Classify & Queue\\n90:     Priority and Fairness-->>Handler Chain: Proceed\\n91:     Handler Chain->>Admission Control: Mutate & Validate\\n92:     Admission Control-->>Handler Chain: Object OK\\n93:     Handler Chain->>REST Endpoint: Handle\\n94:     REST Endpoint-->>Handler Chain: Response\\n95:     Handler Chain-->>Client: Response\\n96: ```\\n97: \\n98: The handler chain consists of the following stages:\\n99: \\n100: 1.  **Authentication (`pkg/authentication`):** This filter identifies the user. The system is\\n101:     pluggable and composed of multiple authenticators (e.g., client certs, bearer tokens, OIDC).\\n102:     The identity of the user is determined by the first authenticator in the chain that successfully\\n103:     identifies the user.\\n104: 2.  **Authorization (`pkg/authorization`):** This filter checks if the user is permitted to\\n105:     perform the action. This system is also pluggable and composed of multiple authorizers\\n106:     (e.g., RBAC, Node, Webhook). Each authorizer may respond with either allow, deny, or no opinion.\\n107:     If the response is no opinion, the request is passed to the next authorizer in the chain.\\n108: 3.  **Priority and Fairness (`pkg/util/flowcontrol`):** This subsystem manages request\\n109:     concurrency, classifying requests into `FlowSchema`s and `PriorityLevel`s to prevent\\n110:     overload. This feature was introduced to prevent high traffic from overwhelming the API\\n111:     server and to ensure that critical cluster operations are not starved.\\n112: 4.  **Admission Control (`pkg/admission`):** This is the primary mechanism for policy\\n113:     enforcement. It is only at this stage that the request body is deserialized into an\\n114:     object. It is a chain of plugins that can mutate or validate an object. The built-in Pod\\n115:     Security admission controller is a key example of this, enforcing Pod Security Standards\\n116:     at the namespace level.\\n117: 5.  **REST Endpoint Handling (`pkg/endpoints`):** The request is finally dispatched to the\\n118:     appropriate REST handler, which is installed by the `APIInstaller`.\\n119: \\n120: ## 3. API Group Registration\\n121: \\n122: The high-level steps for introducing an API are:\\n123: \\n124: 1.  **Define Types:** Create or modify the Go structs in the `types.go` file for the API group.\\n125: 2.  **Generate Code:** Use the code generators provided by the Kubernetes project to create the required\\n126:     boilerplate methods for deep-copy, conversion, and defaulting.\\n127: 3.  **Implement the `Strategy`:** Write the custom business logic and validation for the\\n128:     resource in its `Strategy` object.\\n129: 4.  **Register and Install:** Create the `APIGroupInfo` struct, bundling the `Scheme` and the\\n130:     `Strategy`-configured storage, and pass it to the `GenericAPIServer`'s `InstallAPIGroup`\\n131:     method.\\n132: \\n133: ### The API Group Registry\\n134: \\n135: The `runtime.Scheme` acts as a central registry for an API group's type information. A single\\n136: `Scheme` object is created for each API group and is responsible for the following key\\n137: capabilities:\\n138: \\n139: *   **Type Registration and Mapping:** The `Scheme`'s primary role is to map a GroupVersionKind\\n140:     (GVK) to its corresponding Go type and back. This process also relies on the `deepcopy-gen`\\n141:     tool to create `DeepCopy()` methods for each type, which is critical for ensuring that\\n142:     objects returned from caches are never modified directly.\\n143: \\n144: *   **API Conversion:** The `Scheme` stores the conversion functions that translate objects\\n145:     between different API versions. These functions are typically generated by the\\n146:     `conversion-gen` tool and enable the **hub-and-spoke** model.\\n147: \\n148: *   **Defaulting:** The `Scheme` registers defaulting functions that populate optional fields in\\n149:     an object. These are usually generated by the `defaulter-gen` tool.\\n150: \\n151: *   **Declarative Validation:** The `Scheme` can store and execute code-generated validation\\n152:     functions, providing a baseline level of validation. This is distinct from the primary,\\n153:     handwritten business logic validation, which is handled by the `Strategy` object.\\n154: \\n155: ### The `APIGroupInfo` Struct and `Strategy` Object\\n156: \\n157: With a populated `Scheme`, the API group is registered with the `GenericAPIServer` by bundling\\n158: the `Scheme` with the storage backend and versioning information into an `APIGroupInfo` struct.\\n159: \\n160: ```mermaid\\n161: graph TD\\n162:     subgraph Server Configuration\\n163:         A[APIGroupInfo for apps v1];\\n164:         A --> B{Scheme: Knows Deployment v1};\\n165:         A --> C{Storage: deployments RESTStorage};\\n166:         A --> D{Version Priority: v1, v1beta1};\\n167:     end\\n168: \\n169:     subgraph RESTStorage Implementation\\n170:         C --> E[genericregistry.Store];\\n171:         E --> F[etcd client];\\n172:         E --> G[Deployment Strategy];\\n173:     end\\n174: \\n175:     subgraph Server Runtime\\n176:         H[GenericAPIServer] -- InstallAPIGroup --> I[APIInstaller];\\n177:         I -- Uses --> A;\\n178:         I --> J{Register /apis/apps/v1/deployments};\\n179:         J --> K[HTTP Handler];\\n180:         K -- On Request --> C;\\n181:     end\\n182: ```\\n183: \\n184: The registration process follows these steps:\\n185: \\n186: 1.  **`APIGroupInfo` Construction:** For each API group, an `APIGroupInfo` struct is created,\\n187:     which contains the populated `Scheme`, a map of resources to their storage\\n188:     implementations, and an ordered list of **Version Priority**.\\n189: \\n190: 2.  **REST Storage Instantiation:** For each resource, a `genericregistry.Store` is created. It\\n191:     is configured with a resource-specific `Strategy` object that contains the core business\\n192:     logic (e.g., handwritten validation).\\n193: \\n194: 3.  **API Group Installation:** The `GenericAPIServer`'s `InstallAPIGroup` method takes the\\n195:     `APIGroupInfo` and uses an `APIInstaller` to expose the resources as HTTP endpoints.\\n196: \\n197: ## 4. Watch Cache\\n198: \\n199: To handle the high volume of watch requests from controllers without overwhelming etcd, the\\n200: apiserver uses a **watch cache**. The implementation can be found in\\n201: `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/storage/cacher/`.\\n202: \\n203: *   **Initialization:** The cacher first performs a `LIST` to get the current state of all\\n204:     objects and a `ResourceVersion` for that point-in-time. It then starts a `WATCH` from\\n205:     that version to ensure a consistent stream of events.\\n206: *   **Serving from Cache:** Most list and watch requests are served from this in-memory cache, which\\n207:     dramatically reduces the load on etcd. Consistent reads are also served from the\\n208:     cache. This is achieved by first fetching the revision number of the latest write from\\n209:     etcd. The server then ensures the cache is at least that recent—waiting for it to\\n210:     refresh if necessary—before serving the request.\\n211: *   **Fallback to Storage:** If a client request cannot be served from the\\n212:     cache's buffer, the request \\\"falls through\\\" to the underlying etcd storage.\\n213: *   **Bookmarks:** The cacher uses bookmark events to track the latest `ResourceVersion` for\\n214:     unchanged objects. This prevents the cache's `ResourceVersion` from becoming too old,\\n215:     which avoids the need for expensive relist operations from etcd when the objects have\\n216:     not been modified.\\n217: \\n218: ## 5. Conflict Resolution\\n219: \\n220: *   **Optimistic Concurrency via `resourceVersion`:** Clients are expected to perform updates using a\\n221:     read-modify-write workflow. The apiserver uses the `resourceVersion` field of every\\n222:     object to enforce optimistic concurrency. This `resourceVersion` is not an arbitrary number;\\n223:     it maps directly to etcd's globally consistent `mod_revision`. When a client submits an\\n224:     update (`PUT` or `PATCH`), it must provide the `resourceVersion` of the object it based its\\n225:     modifications on. If the `resourceVersion` on the server does not match the current\\n226:     `mod_revision` in etcd, the server rejects the request with a `409 Conflict` error. This\\n227:     forces the client to re-read the object, resolve the conflict, and resubmit with the new\\n228:     `resourceVersion`.\\n229: *   **Server-Side Apply:** A declarative, \\\"intent-based\\\" patch. The server maintains a\\n230:     `managedFields` section in the object's metadata to track which \\\"manager\\\" (e.g., a\\n231:     controller) owns each field. This allows multiple actors to manage different parts of the\\n232:     same object without overwriting each other's changes.\\n233: \\n234: ## 6. Discovery and OpenAPI\\n235: \\n236: Apiservers serve the `/apis` discovery endpoints and the `/openapi/v2` and `/openapi/v3`\\n237: specifications. The generation of the OpenAPI specification is a multi-stage process.\\n238: \\n239: *   **`openapi-gen`**: This tool reflects on Go structs, reads godoc comments, and looks at\\n240:     validation struct tags to generate a map of all API definitions.\\n241: *   **`zz_generated.openapi.go`**: The output is a large Go file containing a\\n242:     `GetOpenAPIDefinitions` function.\\n243: *   **Runtime**: The `GenericAPIServer` calls this generated function to build the final OpenAPI\\n244:     JSON spec that it serves to clients.\\n245: \\n246: ## 7. Security & Observability\\n247: \\n248: *   **Audit (`pkg/audit`):** The apiserver has a policy-driven event logging pipeline. The audit\\n249:     policy controls what is logged and at which stage of a request.\\n250: *   **Security:**\\n251:     *   **mTLS:** The primary authentication mechanism for system components.\\n252:     *   **Service Account Token Issuance:** The `kube-apiserver` acts as an OIDC provider,\\n253:         issuing and validating JWTs for `ServiceAccount`s.\\n254: \\n255: ## 8. Streaming Protocols\\n256: \\n257: *   **Websockets:** The apiserver uses websockets to upgrade HTTP\\n258:     connections for interactive, streaming protocols like `exec`, `attach`, and\\n259:     `port-forward`. The `UpgradeAwareProxyHandler` manages this process.\\n260: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0@8c9c67c000104450cfc5a5f48053a9a84b73cf93/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/ARCHITECTURE.md\"}"}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"96b17583-ccba-4b45-a8c8-98253d4490f6","uuid":"0cd59711-293b-49f8-b6e0-f95cd6c13ad2","tool_use_result":{"content":"{\"content\":\"1: # apiserver Architecture\\n2: \\n3: ## 1. Server Composition\\n4: \\n5: The `kube-apiserver` binary is not one server, but a **server chain** of three distinct\\n6: `GenericAPIServer` instances: the core Kubernetes API, API extensions (CRDs), and the aggregation\\n7: layer.\\n8: \\n9: This composition is managed by a layered configuration system, starting with command-line\\n10: flags parsed by `options` structs (e.g., `RecommendedOptions` in `pkg/server/options`), which\\n11: populate a `Config` object that is then used to instantiate the `GenericAPIServer`\\n12: instances. The construction of this delegation chain can be found in the `CreateServerChain`\\n13: function in `cmd/kube-apiserver/app/server.go`.\\n14: \\n15: ```mermaid\\n16: graph TD\\n17:     subgraph Incoming Request\\n18:         direction LR\\n19:         A[User/Client] --> B{/apis/apps/v1/deployments};\\n20:     end\\n21: \\n22:     subgraph kube-apiserver process\\n23:         direction LR\\n24:         B --> C[Aggregator Server];\\n25:         C -- Not an APIService --> D[Kube API Server];\\n26:         D -- Handles Request --> E[REST Storage];\\n27:         C -- Is an APIService --> F[Proxy to Extension API Server];\\n28:         D -- Not a Core API --> G[API Extensions Server];\\n29:         G -- Handles CRD --> E;\\n30:     end\\n31: ```\\n32: \\n33: 1.  **Aggregator Server (`kube-aggregator`):**\\n34:     *   **Purpose:** Handles the `apiregistration.k8s.io` API and acts as a reverse proxy for\\n35:         extension API servers. This functionality was designed to allow third-party APIs to be\\n36:         \\\"aggregated\\\" into the main Kubernetes API server seamlessly.\\n37:     *   **Mechanism:** It watches `APIService` objects. When a request arrives (e.g., for\\n38:         `/apis/mycompany.com/v1/myresources`), it checks if an `APIService` has \\\"claimed\\\"\\n39:         that path. If so, it uses a `ServiceResolver` to find the IP of the backing\\n40:         `Service` and proxies the request.\\n41:     *   **Use Case:** This pattern is for programmatic, high-control extensions that require\\n42:         custom business logic (e.g., non-CRUD subresources like `/logs`) or alternative\\n43:         storage backends.\\n44:     *   **Delegation:** If no `APIService` matches, it delegates the request to the next\\n45:         server in the chain.\\n46: \\n47: 2.  **Kube API Server (Core):**\\n48:     *   **Purpose:** Serves all the built-in Kubernetes APIs (`core/v1`, `apps/v1`, etc.).\\n49:     *   **Mechanism:** This is the main server, configured with all the core REST storage\\n50:         strategies.\\n51:     *   **Delegation:** If a request is for a path that is not a core API (e.g., for a CRD),\\n52:         it delegates the request to the next server in the chain.\\n53: \\n54: 3.  **API Extensions Server (`apiextensions-apiserver`):**\\n55:     *   **Purpose:** Handles the `apiextensions.k8s.io` API, which manages\\n56:         `CustomResourceDefinition` objects (CRDs). The evolution of CRDs from a simple extension\\n57:         mechanism to a feature-rich system with validation, versioning, and defaulting is\\n58:         documented in a series of KEPs, starting with the graduation to GA in Kubernetes v1.16.\\n59:     *   **Mechanism:** When a CRD is created, this server dynamically creates and installs a\\n60:         new REST storage handler for the new resource, making it immediately available.\\n61:     *   **Use Case:** CRDs are the most common extension pattern, offering a declarative,\\n62:         schema-based way to define new resource types that are stored in etcd and require no\\n63:         custom API server code.\\n64:     *   **Delegation:** It is the end of the chain. If it cannot handle a request, a `404 Not\\n65:         Found` is returned.\\n66: \\n67: ## 2. Handler Chain\\n68: \\n69: Every request flows through a standard chain of HTTP handlers (filters). The request body\\n70: is not deserialized until it has passed authentication and authorization. The default handler\\n71: chain is constructed by the `DefaultBuildHandlerChain` function in\\n72: `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/config.go`.\\n73: \\n74: ```mermaid\\n75: sequenceDiagram\\n76:     participant Client\\n77:     participant Handler Chain\\n78:     participant Authentication\\n79:     participant Authorization\\n80:     participant Priority and Fairness\\n81:     participant Admission Control\\n82:     participant REST Endpoint\\n83: \\n84:     Client->>Handler Chain: Request\\n85:     Handler Chain->>Authentication: Authenticate\\n86:     Authentication-->>Handler Chain: User Info\\n87:     Handler Chain->>Authorization: Authorize\\n88:     Authorization-->>Handler Chain: Allowed/Denied\\n89:     Handler Chain->>Priority and Fairness: Classify & Queue\\n90:     Priority and Fairness-->>Handler Chain: Proceed\\n91:     Handler Chain->>Admission Control: Mutate & Validate\\n92:     Admission Control-->>Handler Chain: Object OK\\n93:     Handler Chain->>REST Endpoint: Handle\\n94:     REST Endpoint-->>Handler Chain: Response\\n95:     Handler Chain-->>Client: Response\\n96: ```\\n97: \\n98: The handler chain consists of the following stages:\\n99: \\n100: 1.  **Authentication (`pkg/authentication`):** This filter identifies the user. The system is\\n101:     pluggable and composed of multiple authenticators (e.g., client certs, bearer tokens, OIDC).\\n102:     The identity of the user is determined by the first authenticator in the chain that successfully\\n103:     identifies the user.\\n104: 2.  **Authorization (`pkg/authorization`):** This filter checks if the user is permitted to\\n105:     perform the action. This system is also pluggable and composed of multiple authorizers\\n106:     (e.g., RBAC, Node, Webhook). Each authorizer may respond with either allow, deny, or no opinion.\\n107:     If the response is no opinion, the request is passed to the next authorizer in the chain.\\n108: 3.  **Priority and Fairness (`pkg/util/flowcontrol`):** This subsystem manages request\\n109:     concurrency, classifying requests into `FlowSchema`s and `PriorityLevel`s to prevent\\n110:     overload. This feature was introduced to prevent high traffic from overwhelming the API\\n111:     server and to ensure that critical cluster operations are not starved.\\n112: 4.  **Admission Control (`pkg/admission`):** This is the primary mechanism for policy\\n113:     enforcement. It is only at this stage that the request body is deserialized into an\\n114:     object. It is a chain of plugins that can mutate or validate an object. The built-in Pod\\n115:     Security admission controller is a key example of this, enforcing Pod Security Standards\\n116:     at the namespace level.\\n117: 5.  **REST Endpoint Handling (`pkg/endpoints`):** The request is finally dispatched to the\\n118:     appropriate REST handler, which is installed by the `APIInstaller`.\\n119: \\n120: ## 3. API Group Registration\\n121: \\n122: The high-level steps for introducing an API are:\\n123: \\n124: 1.  **Define Types:** Create or modify the Go structs in the `types.go` file for the API group.\\n125: 2.  **Generate Code:** Use the code generators provided by the Kubernetes project to create the required\\n126:     boilerplate methods for deep-copy, conversion, and defaulting.\\n127: 3.  **Implement the `Strategy`:** Write the custom business logic and validation for the\\n128:     resource in its `Strategy` object.\\n129: 4.  **Register and Install:** Create the `APIGroupInfo` struct, bundling the `Scheme` and the\\n130:     `Strategy`-configured storage, and pass it to the `GenericAPIServer`'s `InstallAPIGroup`\\n131:     method.\\n132: \\n133: ### The API Group Registry\\n134: \\n135: The `runtime.Scheme` acts as a central registry for an API group's type information. When a request arrives (e.g., for\n38:         `/apis/mycompany.com/v1/myresources`), it checks if an `APIService` has \"claimed\"\n39:         that path. If so, it uses a `ServiceResolver` to find the IP of the backing\n40:         `Service` and proxies the request.\n41:     *   **Use Case:** This pattern is for programmatic, high-control extensions that require\n42:         custom business logic (e.g., non-CRUD subresources like `/logs`) or alternative\n43:         storage backends.\n44:     *   **Delegation:** If no `APIService` matches, it delegates the request to the next\n45:         server in the chain.\n46: \n47: 2.  **Kube API Server (Core):**\n48:     *   **Purpose:** Serves all the built-in Kubernetes APIs (`core/v1`, `apps/v1`, etc.).\n49:     *   **Mechanism:** This is the main server, configured with all the core REST storage\n50:         strategies.\n51:     *   **Delegation:** If a request is for a path that is not a core API (e.g., for a CRD),\n52:         it delegates the request to the next server in the chain.\n53: \n54: 3.  **API Extensions Server (`apiextensions-apiserver`):**\n55:     *   **Purpose:** Handles the `apiextensions.k8s.io` API, which manages\n56:         `CustomResourceDefinition` objects (CRDs). The evolution of CRDs from a simple extension\n57:         mechanism to a feature-rich system with validation, versioning, and defaulting is\n58:         documented in a series of KEPs, starting with the graduation to GA in Kubernetes v1.16.\n59:     *   **Mechanism:** When a CRD is created, this server dynamically creates and installs a\n60:         new REST storage handler for the new resource, making it immediately available.\n61:     *   **Use Case:** CRDs are the most common extension pattern, offering a declarative,\n62:         schema-based way to define new resource types that are stored in etcd and require no\n63:         custom API server code.\n64:     *   **Delegation:** It is the end of the chain. If it cannot handle a request, a `404 Not\n65:         Found` is returned.\n66: \n67: ## 2. Handler Chain\n68: \n69: Every request flows through a standard chain of HTTP handlers (filters). The request body\n70: is not deserialized until it has passed authentication and authorization. The default handler\n71: chain is constructed by the `DefaultBuildHandlerChain` function in\n72: `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/config.go`.\n73: \n74: ```mermaid\n75: sequenceDiagram\n76:     participant Client\n77:     participant Handler Chain\n78:     participant Authentication\n79:     participant Authorization\n80:     participant Priority and Fairness\n81:     participant Admission Control\n82:     participant REST Endpoint\n83: \n84:     Client->>Handler Chain: Request\n85:     Handler Chain->>Authentication: Authenticate\n86:     Authentication-->>Handler Chain: User Info\n87:     Handler Chain->>Authorization: Authorize\n88:     Authorization-->>Handler Chain: Allowed/Denied\n89:     Handler Chain->>Priority and Fairness: Classify & Queue\n90:     Priority and Fairness-->>Handler Chain: Proceed\n91:     Handler Chain->>Admission Control: Mutate & Validate\n92:     Admission Control-->>Handler Chain: Object OK\n93:     Handler Chain->>REST Endpoint: Handle\n94:     REST Endpoint-->>Handler Chain: Response\n95:     Handler Chain-->>Client: Response\n96: ```\n97: \n98: The handler chain consists of the following stages:\n99: \n100: 1.  **Authentication (`pkg/authentication`):** This filter identifies the user. The system is\n101:     pluggable and composed of multiple authenticators (e.g., client certs, bearer tokens, OIDC).\n102:     The identity of the user is determined by the first authenticator in the chain that successfully\n103:     identifies the user.\n104: 2.  **Authorization (`pkg/authorization`):** This filter checks if the user is permitted to\n105:     perform the action. This system is also pluggable and composed of multiple authorizers\n106:     (e.g., RBAC, Node, Webhook). Each authorizer may respond with either allow, deny, or no opinion.\n107:     If the response is no opinion, the request is passed to the next authorizer in the chain.\n108: 3.  **Priority and Fairness (`pkg/util/flowcontrol`):** This subsystem manages request\n109:     concurrency, classifying requests into `FlowSchema`s and `PriorityLevel`s to prevent\n110:     overload. This feature was introduced to prevent high traffic from overwhelming the API\n111:     server and to ensure that critical cluster operations are not starved.\n112: 4.  **Admission Control (`pkg/admission`):** This is the primary mechanism for policy\n113:     enforcement. It is only at this stage that the request body is deserialized into an\n114:     object. It is a chain of plugins that can mutate or validate an object. The built-in Pod\n115:     Security admission controller is a key example of this, enforcing Pod Security Standards\n116:     at the namespace level.\n117: 5.  **REST Endpoint Handling (`pkg/endpoints`):** The request is finally dispatched to the\n118:     appropriate REST handler, which is installed by the `APIInstaller`.\n119: \n120: ## 3. API Group Registration\n121: \n122: The high-level steps for introducing an API are:\n123: \n124: 1.  **Define Types:** Create or modify the Go structs in the `types.go` file for the API group.\n125: 2.  **Generate Code:** Use the code generators provided by the Kubernetes project to create the required\n126:     boilerplate methods for deep-copy, conversion, and defaulting.\n127: 3.  **Implement the `Strategy`:** Write the custom business logic and validation for the\n128:     resource in its `Strategy` object.\n129: 4.  **Register and Install:** Create the `APIGroupInfo` struct, bundling the `Scheme` and the\n130:     `Strategy`-configured storage, and pass it to the `GenericAPIServer`'s `InstallAPIGroup`\n131:     method.\n132: \n133: ### The API Group Registry\n134: \n135: The `runtime.Scheme` acts as a central registry for an API group's type information. A single\n136: `Scheme` object is created for each API group and is responsible for the following key\n137: capabilities:\n138: \n139: *   **Type Registration and Mapping:** The `Scheme`'s primary role is to map a GroupVersionKind\n140:     (GVK) to its corresponding Go type and back. This process also relies on the `deepcopy-gen`\n141:     tool to create `DeepCopy()` methods for each type, which is critical for ensuring that\n142:     objects returned from caches are never modified directly.\n143: \n144: *   **API Conversion:** The `Scheme` stores the conversion functions that translate objects\n145:     between different API versions. These functions are typically generated by the\n146:     `conversion-gen` tool and enable the **hub-and-spoke** model.\n147: \n148: *   **Defaulting:** The `Scheme` registers defaulting functions that populate optional fields in\n149:     an object. These are usually generated by the `defaulter-gen` tool.\n150: \n151: *   **Declarative Validation:** The `Scheme` can store and execute code-generated validation\n152:     functions, providing a baseline level of validation. This is distinct from the primary,\n153:     handwritten business logic validation, which is handled by the `Strategy` object.\n154: \n155: ### The `APIGroupInfo` Struct and `Strategy` Object\n156: \n157: With a populated `Scheme`, the API group is registered with the `GenericAPIServer` by bundling\n158: the `Scheme` with the storage backend and versioning information into an `APIGroupInfo` struct.\n159: \n160: ```mermaid\n161: graph TD\n162:     subgraph Server Configuration\n163:         A[APIGroupInfo for apps v1];\n164:         A --> B{Scheme: Knows Deployment v1};\n165:         A --> C{Storage: deployments RESTStorage};\n166:         A --> D{Version Priority: v1, v1beta1};\n167:     end\n168: \n169:     subgraph RESTStorage Implementation\n170:         C --> E[genericregistry.Store];\n171:         E --> F[etcd client];\n172:         E --> G[Deployment Strategy];\n173:     end\n174: \n175:     subgraph Server Runtime\n176:         H[GenericAPIServer] -- InstallAPIGroup --> I[APIInstaller];\n177:         I -- Uses --> A;\n178:         I --> J{Register /apis/apps/v1/deployments};\n179:         J --> K[HTTP Handler];\n180:         K -- On Request --> C;\n181:     end\n182: ```\n183: \n184: The registration process follows these steps:\n185: \n186: 1.  **`APIGroupInfo` Construction:** For each API group, an `APIGroupInfo` struct is created,\n187:     which contains the populated `Scheme`, a map of resources to their storage\n188:     implementations, and an ordered list of **Version Priority**.\n189: \n190: 2.  **REST Storage Instantiation:** For each resource, a `genericregistry.Store` is created. It\n191:     is configured with a resource-specific `Strategy` object that contains the core business\n192:     logic (e.g., handwritten validation).\n193: \n194: 3.  **API Group Installation:** The `GenericAPIServer`'s `InstallAPIGroup` method takes the\n195:     `APIGroupInfo` and uses an `APIInstaller` to expose the resources as HTTP endpoints.\n196: \n197: ## 4. Watch Cache\n198: \n199: To handle the high volume of watch requests from controllers without overwhelming etcd, the\n200: apiserver uses a **watch cache**. The implementation can be found in\n201: `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/storage/cacher/`.\n202: \n203: *   **Initialization:** The cacher first performs a `LIST` to get the current state of all\n204:     objects and a `ResourceVersion` for that point-in-time. It then starts a `WATCH` from\n205:     that version to ensure a consistent stream of events.\n206: *   **Serving from Cache:** Most list and watch requests are served from this in-memory cache, which\n207:     dramatically reduces the load on etcd. Consistent reads are also served from the\n208:     cache. This is achieved by first fetching the revision number of the latest write from\n209:     etcd. The server then ensures the cache is at least that recent—waiting for it to\n210:     refresh if necessary—before serving the request.\n211: *   **Fallback to Storage:** If a client request cannot be served from the\n212:     cache's buffer, the request \"falls through\" to the underlying etcd storage.\n213: *   **Bookmarks:** The cacher uses bookmark events to track the latest `ResourceVersion` for\n214:     unchanged objects. This prevents the cache's `ResourceVersion` from becoming too old,\n215:     which avoids the need for expensive relist operations from etcd when the objects have\n216:     not been modified.\n217: \n218: ## 5. Conflict Resolution\n219: \n220: *   **Optimistic Concurrency via `resourceVersion`:** Clients are expected to perform updates using a\n221:     read-modify-write workflow. The apiserver uses the `resourceVersion` field of every\n222:     object to enforce optimistic concurrency. This `resourceVersion` is not an arbitrary number;\n223:     it maps directly to etcd's globally consistent `mod_revision`. When a client submits an\n224:     update (`PUT` or `PATCH`), it must provide the `resourceVersion` of the object it based its\n225:     modifications on. If the `resourceVersion` on the server does not match the current\n226:     `mod_revision` in etcd, the server rejects the request with a `409 Conflict` error. This\n227:     forces the client to re-read the object, resolve the conflict, and resubmit with the new\n228:     `resourceVersion`.\n229: *   **Server-Side Apply:** A declarative, \"intent-based\" patch. The server maintains a\n230:     `managedFields` section in the object's metadata to track which \"manager\" (e.g., a\n231:     controller) owns each field. This allows multiple actors to manage different parts of the\n232:     same object without overwriting each other's changes.\n233: \n234: ## 6. Discovery and OpenAPI\n235: \n236: Apiservers serve the `/apis` discovery endpoints and the `/openapi/v2` and `/openapi/v3`\n237: specifications. The generation of the OpenAPI specification is a multi-stage process.\n238: \n239: *   **`openapi-gen`**: This tool reflects on Go structs, reads godoc comments, and looks at\n240:     validation struct tags to generate a map of all API definitions.\n241: *   **`zz_generated.openapi.go`**: The output is a large Go file containing a\n242:     `GetOpenAPIDefinitions` function.\n243: *   **Runtime**: The `GenericAPIServer` calls this generated function to build the final OpenAPI\n244:     JSON spec that it serves to clients.\n245: \n246: ## 7. Security & Observability\n247: \n248: *   **Audit (`pkg/audit`):** The apiserver has a policy-driven event logging pipeline. The audit\n249:     policy controls what is logged and at which stage of a request.\n250: *   **Security:**\n251:     *   **mTLS:** The primary authentication mechanism for system components.\n252:     *   **Service Account Token Issuance:** The `kube-apiserver` acts as an OIDC provider,\n253:         issuing and validating JWTs for `ServiceAccount`s.\n254: \n255: ## 8. Streaming Protocols\n256: \n257: *   **Websockets:** The apiserver uses websockets to upgrade HTTP\n258:     connections for interactive, streaming protocols like `exec`, `attach`, and\n259:     `port-forward`. The `UpgradeAwareProxyHandler` manages this process.\n260: ","url":"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0@8c9c67c000104450cfc5a5f48053a9a84b73cf93/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/ARCHITECTURE.md"}}}

It's first consumers are\n15: `k8s.io/kubernetes`, `k8s.io/kube-aggregator`, and `github.com/kubernetes-incubator/service-catalog`.\n16: \n17: \n18: ## Compatibility\n19: \n20: There are *NO compatibility guarantees* for this repository, yet. On a not found error, will either\n197: \t// return a zero object of the requested type, or an error, depending on 'opts.ignoreNotFound'.\n198: \t// Treats empty responses and nil response nodes exactly like a not found error.\n199: \t// The returned contents may be delayed, but it is guaranteed that they will\n200: \t// match 'opts.ResourceVersion' according 'opts.ResourceVersionMatch'.\n201: \tGet(ctx context.Context, key string, opts GetOptions, objPtr runtime.Object) error\n202: \n203: \t// GetList unmarshalls objects found at key into a *List api object (an object\n204: \t// that satisfies runtime.IsList definition).\n205: \t// If 'opts.Recursive' is false, 'key' is used as an exact match. If `opts.Recursive'\n206: \t// is true, 'key' is used as a prefix.\n207: \t// The returned contents may be delayed, but it is guaranteed that they will\n208: \t// match 'opts.ResourceVersion' according 'opts.ResourceVersionMatch'.\n209: \tGetList(ctx context.Context, key string, opts ListOptions, listObj runtime.Object) error\n210: \n211: \t// GuaranteedUpdate keeps calling 'tryUpdate()' to update key 'key' (of type 'destination')\n212: \t// retrying the update until success if there is index conflict.\n213: \t// Note that object passed to tryUpdate may change across invocations of tryUpdate() if\n214: \t// other writers are simultaneously updating it, so tryUpdate() needs to take into account\n215: \t// the current contents of the object when deciding how the update object should look.\n216: \t// If the key doesn't exist, it will return NotFound storage error if ignoreNotFound=false\n217: \t// else `destination` will be set to the zero value of it's type.\n218: \t// If the eventual successful invocation of `tryUpdate` returns an output with the same serialized\n219: \t// contents as the input, it won't perform any update, but instead set `destination` to an object with those\n220: \t// contents.\n221: \t// If 'cachedExistingObject' is non-nil, it can be used as a suggestion about the\n222: \t// current version of the object to avoid read operation from storage to get it.\n223: \t// However, the implementations have to retry in case suggestion is stale.\n224: \t//\n225: \t// Example:\n226: \t//\n227: \t// s := /* implementation of Interface */\n228: \t// err := s.GuaranteedUpdate(\n229: \t//     \"myKey\", &MyType{}, true, preconditions,\n230: \t//     func(input runtime.Object, res ResponseMeta) (runtime.Object, *uint64, error) {\n231: \t//       // Before each invocation of the user defined function, \"input\" is reset to\n232: \t//       // current contents for \"myKey\" in database.\n233: \t//       curr := input.(*MyType)  // Guaranteed to succeed.\n234: \t//\n235: \t//       // Make the modification\n236: \t//       curr.Counter++\n237: \t//\n238: \t//       // Return the modified object - return an error to stop iterating. Return\n239: \t//       // a uint64 to alter the TTL on the object, or nil to keep it the same value.\n240: \t//       return cur, nil, nil\n241: \t//    }, cachedExistingObject\n242: \t// )\n243: \tGuaranteedUpdate(\n244: \t\tctx context.Context, key string, destination runtime.Object, ignoreNotFound bool,\n245: \t\tpreconditions *Preconditions, tryUpdate UpdateFunc, cachedExistingObject runtime.Object) error\n246: \n247: \t// Stats returns storage stats.\n248: \tStats(ctx context.Context) (Stats, error)\n249: \n250: \t// ReadinessCheck checks if the storage is ready for accepting requests.\n251: \tReadinessCheck() error\n252: \n253: \t// RequestWatchProgress requests the a watch stream progress status be sent in the\n254: \t// watch response stream as soon as possible.\n255: \t// Used for monitor watch progress even if watching resources with no changes.\n256: \t//\n257: \t// If watch is lagging, progress status might:\n258: \t// * be pointing to stale resource version. {"type":"assistant","message":{"model":"claude-haiku-4-5-20251001","id":"msg_01FXkPxiTA26PF7mCfZwyePb","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"tool_use","id":"toolu_016ikLPhEuuCBQioNhmfNYny","name":"Write","input":{"file_path":"/workspace/documentation.md","content":"# Kubernetes API Server Library: Architecture and Extension Guide\n\n## 1. Subsystem Overview\n\nThe Kubernetes API server library (`k8s.io/apiserver`) is a reusable framework for building Kubernetes-compatible REST APIs. It is the core component used by `kube-apiserver`, `kube-aggregator`, and `apiextensions-apiserver`. The library provides:\n\n- **Request handling pipeline**: Authentication, authorization, admission control, and request dispatching\n- **Storage abstraction**: Unified interface for persisting and retrieving objects via etcd\n- **API group registration**: Mechanism for exposing resource types via REST endpoints\n- **Watch/streaming**: Long-running connections for real-time change notifications\n- **Discovery and OpenAPI**: Standards-compliant API documentation\n\n### Boundaries and Dependencies\n\n**Upstream dependencies:**\n- `k8s.io/apimachinery`: Core types (runtime.Object, schema definitions, codecs)\n- `k8s.io/client-go`: Authentication, transport utilities\n- `k8s.io/component-base`: Logging, tracing, metrics, feature gates\n- `go.etcd.io/etcd/client`: etcd3 backend connectivity\n\n**Downstream consumers:**\n- `k8s.io/kubernetes`: Main Kubernetes distribution packages apiserver as a library\n- `k8s.io/kube-aggregator`: Proxies requests to third-party API servers\n- Custom API servers: Projects extending Kubernetes with their own resources\n\n---\n\n## 2. Key Components and Responsibilities\n\n### 2.1 GenericAPIServer\n\n**File**: `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/genericapiserver.go`\n\nThe `GenericAPIServer` is the core runtime object representing a single API server instance. It:\n\n- Owns the HTTP mux and handler chain\n- Manages registered API groups and their storage backends\n- Provides methods for runtime operations (graceful shutdown, health checks)\n- Maintains discovery information about all registered APIs\n\n**Key fields:**\n```go\nHandler *APIServerHandler          // HTTP request router and middleware\nadmissionControl admission.Interface // Policy enforcement pipeline\nSerializer runtime.NegotiatedSerializer // Codec for encoding/decoding\nSecureServingInfo *SecureServingInfo  // TLS configuration\n```\n\n### 2.2 Server Chain Architecture\n\n**File**: `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/ARCHITECTURE.md`\n\nThe `kube-apiserver` binary comprises three chained `GenericAPIServer` instances:\n\n1. **Aggregator Server** (`/apiregistration.k8s.io`)\n   - Watches `APIService` objects to discover third-party APIs\n   - Proxies requests matching an APIService to external servers via `ServiceResolver`\n   - Returns 404 if no match; delegates to next server\n\n2. **Core API Server** (`/api/v1`, `/apis/{group}/{version}`)\n   - Serves built-in Kubernetes resources (Pods, Services, Deployments, etc.)\n   - If a request doesn't match core APIs, delegates to extensions server\n\n3. **Extensions Server** (`/apis/apiextensions.k8s.io`)\n   - Manages `CustomResourceDefinition` (CRD) objects\n   - Dynamically creates REST handlers for CRD-defined resources\n   - End of chain; returns 404 if unmatched\n\n### 2.3 Request Handler Chain\n\n**File**: `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/config.go` (`DefaultBuildHandlerChain`)\n\nEvery incoming HTTP request traverses a middleware chain **before** reaching API handlers:\n\n1. **AuditInit** – Initialize audit logging context\n2. **PanicRecovery** – Catch and log panics\n3. **RequestInfo** – Extract metadata (verb, resource, subresource) from URL\n4. **MuxAndDiscoveryComplete** – Wait for mux initialization to finish\n5. **RequestReceivedTimestamp** – Record request arrival time\n6. **WarningRecorder** – Wrap response writer to capture warnings\n7. **TimeoutForNonLongRunning** – Apply deadline for short-lived requests (list, get, create)\n8. **RequestDeadline** – Enforce request timeout; record in audit\n9. **MaxInFlightLimit** or **PriorityAndFairness** – Admission by concurrency class\n10. **Impersonation** – Allow impersonating another user (if authorized)\n11. **Audit** – Log request/response per policy\n12. **Authentication** – Identify the user via certificates, tokens, OIDC, etc.\n13. **CORS** – Validate origin and set CORS headers\n14. **Authorization** – Check if user is permitted for the requested action\n15. **REST Endpoint Handler** – Dispatch to the appropriate storage handler (Create, Get, Update, Delete, Watch, etc.)\n\n**Critical design decision**: Request bodies are **not deserialized** until after authorization succeeds. This is a security measure to reject unauthenticated/unauthorized requests early, avoiding expensive JSON parsing.\n\n### 2.4 Authentication and Authorization\n\n**Files**:\n- `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/authentication/`\n- `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/authorization/`\n\n**Authentication**:\n- Pluggable interface: `authenticator.Token` transforms a token into a `user.Info`\n- Multiple authenticators are chained; first successful match wins\n- Common implementations: X.509 certs, bearer tokens (ServiceAccount JWTs, OIDC), webhook\n\n**Authorization**:\n- Pluggable interface: `authorizer.Authorizer` returns `{Allowed, Denied, NoOpinion}`\n- Multiple authorizers chained; any Deny rejects; otherwise allow\n- Common implementations: RBAC (`ClusterRole`/`Role` + bindings), Node (kubelet-specific), Webhook\n\nBoth systems are **decision-only**; they don't mutate objects. Mutation happens in admission.\n\n### 2.5 Admission Control\n\n**File**: `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/admission/`\n\nAdmission controllers are the primary policy enforcement mechanism. They:\n\n- Run **after** authorization succeeds\n- Have **full access** to the deserialized request object\n- Can **mutate** (MutatingAdmissionWebhook, initialization) or **validate** (ValidatingAdmissionPolicy, CEL rules)\n- Are organized in a chain; mutators run first, then validators\n\n**Admission flow** (from `handlers/create.go`):\n\n```\n1. Deserialize request body\n2. Run MutatingAdmissionWebhook plugins (in-tree and webhooks)\n3. Apply managed fields initialization\n4. Call storage.Create() with validation function wrapping ValidatingAdmissionPolicy\n5. Mutate (apply defaults, set ownership, generate names, etc.)\n6. Validate (CEL rules, webhook validations)\n7. Persist to storage\n```\n\n**Built-in plugins:**\n- **PodSecurityPolicy/PodSecurityStandards**: Enforce pod security constraints\n- **ResourceQuota**: Track and limit resource consumption per namespace\n- **ServiceAccount**: Auto-mount token for pods\n- **Namespace Lifecycle**: Prevent creation in terminating namespaces\n- **Webhook**: Generic in-process admission webhooks\n\n### 2.6 API Group Registration\n\n**Files**:\n- `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/genericapiserver.go` (`APIGroupInfo`)\n- `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/endpoints/installer.go` (REST route installation)\n\nEach API group (e.g., `apps`, `rbac.authorization.k8s.io`, `custom.io`) is registered via:\n\n1. **Define types** in Go structs with `+k8s:deepcopy-gen`, `+k8s:conversion-gen` tags\n2. **Create a Scheme** (`runtime.Scheme`) that maps GroupVersionKind ↔ Go type\n3. **Implement a Strategy** (e.g., `Pods`) with resource-specific logic:\n   - `Validate(ctx, obj)` – Enforce invariants\n   - `PrepareForCreate()`, `PrepareForUpdate()` – Normalize object state\n   - `NamespaceScoped()` – Indicate if cluster or namespace-level\n4. **Wrap Storage** with a `genericregistry.Store` configured with the Strategy\n5. **Bundle into APIGroupInfo** and call `GenericAPIServer.InstallAPIGroup()`\n\nThe `Scheme` itself performs:\n- Type registration and lookup\n- Conversion between API versions (hub-and-spoke model)\n- Defaulting (via generated functions or custom logic)\n- Structural validation (via generated functions or CEL expressions)\n\n### 2.7 Storage Architecture\n\n**Files**:\n- `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/storage/interfaces.go` (storage.Interface)\n- `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/storage/cacher/cacher.go` (watch cache)\n- `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/storage/etcd3/` (etcd backend)\n- `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/registry/generic/registry/` (REST adapter)\n\nStorage is organized in layers:\n\n```\nREST Handler (Create, Get, Update, Delete, List, Watch)\n    ↓\ngenericregistry.Store (implements rest.Storage interface)\n    ↓\nCacher (in-memory watch cache with etcd fallback)\n    ↓\netcd3.Store (persistent backend)\n```\n\n**Core interface** (`storage.Interface`):\n- `Create(key, obj, ttl)` – Create new object\n- `Get(key)` – Retrieve by key\n- `GetList(key, prefix, selector)` – List objects matching label/field selectors\n- `GuaranteedUpdate(key, tryUpdate)` – Atomic read-modify-write with retry on conflict\n- `Delete(key, preconditions)` – Remove object (supports grace periods)\n- `Watch(key, resourceVersion)` – Stream changes\n- `Versioner()` – Access to the version abstraction\n\n**Cacher layer** (`storage.cacher.Cacher`):\n- Maintains an in-memory watch cache for all objects of a resource type\n- Initial LIST populates cache; subsequent WATCH streams incremental changes\n- Serves most read requests from cache (dramatic reduction in etcd load)\n- Consistent reads: ensures cache has caught up to requested resourceVersion before returning\n- Uses bookmarks to prevent cache expiry for unchanged objects\n- Falls through to etcd for cache misses or very old resourceVersions\n\n**Conflict resolution** via optimistic concurrency:\n- Every object has a `resourceVersion` field tied to etcd's `mod_revision`\n- Clients perform read-modify-write with client-provided `resourceVersion` as precondition\n- If precondition fails (object changed), server returns HTTP 409 Conflict\n- Server-side apply (`managedFields`) allows multiple controllers to safely manage different fields\n\n---\n\n## 3. End-to-End Request Flow with Evidence\n\n### Example: Creating a Pod\n\n**Request**: `POST /api/v1/namespaces/default/pods`\n\n**Incoming flow**:\n\n1. **Network** → HTTP server binds to port 6443 (KAPI_PORT)\n\n2. **Handler chain** (applies to all requests):\n   ```go\n   // From DefaultBuildHandlerChain in config.go\n   handler = WithPanicRecovery(handler)              // Line 1094\n   handler = WithRequestInfo(handler, ...)            // Line 1091\n   handler = WithAuthentication(handler, ...)         // Line 1056\n   handler = WithAuthorization(handler, ...)          // Line 1019\n   handler = WithPriorityAndFairness(handler, ...)    // Line 1027\n   handler = WithAudit(handler, ...)                  // Line 1043\n   ```\n\n3. **Authentication** (see `pkg/authentication/request/union/union.go`):\n   - TLS client cert authentication extracts certificate subject\n   - Bearer token authentication validates ServiceAccount JWT\n   - First successful authenticator populates `request.UserFrom(ctx)`\n   - If none succeed, handler returns 401 Unauthorized\n\n4. **Authorization** (see `pkg/authorization/union/union.go`):\n   ```go\n   // Check if user can 'create' 'pods' in 'default' namespace\n   if !authorizer.Authorize(user, \"create\", \"pods\", \"default\", nil) {\n       return 403 Forbidden\n   }\n   ```\n\n5. **Priority and Fairness** (see `pkg/util/flowcontrol/`):\n   - Requests classified into `FlowSchema` (based on user, priority)\n   - Assigned to `PriorityLevel` (system-critical vs. user-initiated)\n   - Queued and executed respecting concurrency limits\n   - Prevents thundering herd; ensures core system operations aren't starved\n\n6. **Request routing** (see `endpoints/installer.go`):\n   - URL `/api/v1/namespaces/default/pods` matches the POST action for Pods\n   - Routes to `createHandler` in `endpoints/handlers/create.go`\n\n7. **Create handler** processes the request:\n   ```go\n   // From create.go:53-237\n   func createHandler(r rest.NamedCreater, scope *RequestScope, admit admission.Interface, includeName bool) http.HandlerFunc {\n       return func(w http.ResponseWriter, req *http.Request) {\n           // Extract namespace, name from URL\n           namespace, name, err := scope.Namer.Name(req)  // Line 61\n\n           // Deserialize request body (JSON → Pod object)\n           decoder := scope.Serializer.DecoderToVersion(...)\n           obj, gvk, err := decoder.Decode(body, &defaultGVK, original)  // Line 127\n\n           // Build admission attributes (what resource, user, operation)\n           admissionAttributes := admission.NewAttributesRecord(\n               obj, nil, scope.Kind, namespace, name,\n               scope.Resource, scope.Subresource,\n               admission.Create, options, dryrun.IsDryRun(options.DryRun), userInfo)  // Line 182\n\n           // Call storage layer with admission validation\n           result, err := finisher.FinishRequest(ctx, func() (runtime.Object, error) {\n               // Apply field manager defaults\n               liveObj, _ := scope.Creater.New(scope.Kind)\n               obj = scope.FieldManager.UpdateNoErrors(liveObj, obj, ...)\n\n               // Run mutating admission (webhooks, built-in controllers)\n               if mutatingAdmission, ok := admit.(admission.MutationInterface); ok {\n                   if err := mutatingAdmission.Admit(ctx, admissionAttributes, scope); err != nil {\n                       return nil, err  // Line 203\n                   }\n               }\n\n               // Write to storage\n               result, err := r.Create(ctx, name, obj,\n                   rest.AdmissionToValidateObjectFunc(admit, admissionAttributes, scope),\n                   options)  // Line 184-190\n               return result, err\n           })  // Line 194\n\n           // Write HTTP response (201 Created)\n           transformResponseObject(ctx, scope, req, w, http.StatusCreated, outputMediaType, result)\n       }\n   }\n   ```\n\n8. **Storage layer** (see `registry/generic/registry/store.go`):\n   - `Store.Create()` wraps the actual etcd write\n   - Validates object using admission controller's validation rules\n   - Checks preconditions (no conflicts, valid namespace, etc.)\n   - Calls `storage.Create(key, obj, ttl)` on underlying etcd backend\n\n9. **Etcd persistence** (see `storage/etcd3/store.go`):\n   - Serializes object (JSON or protobuf)\n   - Applies encryption if configured\n   - Writes to etcd at key `/pods/default/{name}`\n   - Etcd assigns a `mod_revision` (becomes `resourceVersion` in returned object)\n   - Returns the persisted object\n\n10. **Response**:\n    - `201 Created` with JSON-serialized Pod object\n    - Includes `metadata.uid`, `metadata.resourceVersion`, `metadata.creationTimestamp`\n\n### Example: Watching Pods\n\n**Request**: `GET /api/v1/namespaces/default/pods?watch=true`\n\n**Flow**:\n\n1. **Handler chain** runs (auth, authz, flowcontrol, etc.)\n2. **Watch handler** (see `endpoints/handlers/watch.go`) detects `?watch=true`\n3. **Cacher.Watch()** is called:\n   ```go\n   // From cacher/cacher.go\n   func (c *Cacher) Watch(ctx context.Context, key string, opts storage.ListOptions) (watch.Interface, error) {\n       // Return a watch.Interface that streams from the in-memory cache\n       w := &cacheWatcher{}\n       c.watcherBuffer.Add(w)  // Add watcher to the buffer\n       return w, nil\n   }\n   ```\n\n4. **Cacher event loop**:\n   - Watches etcd for changes on `{resourcePrefix}` at the given `resourceVersion`\n   - When change arrives, broadcasts to all registered watchers\n   - Sends \"bookmark\" events periodically to keep watches alive\n\n5. **Client stream**:\n   - HTTP connection upgraded to WebSocket (or HTTP chunked transfer encoding)\n   - Client receives event stream (ADDED, MODIFIED, DELETED events)\n   - Connection stays open until client closes or timeout\n\n---\n\n## 4. Failure Modes, Limits, and Design Tradeoffs\n\n### 4.1 Optimistic Concurrency – The resourceVersion Gamble\n\n**Tradeoff**: Simple but requires client cooperation.\n\n- **Good**: No server-side locking needed; scales linearly with clients; stateless reads\n- **Bad**: Clients must retry on 409 Conflict; can starve fast updaters if they always conflict\n\n**Evidence**: `storage/interfaces.go` L138-165 (`Preconditions.Check()`) validates preconditions.\n\n### 4.2 Watch Cache Expiry\n\n**Scenario**: Etcd leader fails; new leader's earliest event is at `revision 10000`. A client watcher still expects revision 5000.\n\n**Resolution** (from `ARCHITECTURE.md` L213-216):\n- Cacher uses \"bookmarks\" (synthetic events) to prevent cache expiry\n- If cache is missing history, request falls through to etcd (which may also fail if revision too old)\n- Client is returned an error; must do a fresh LIST to recover\n\n**Limit**: `DefaultEventFreshDuration` (~75s by default) determines how long events are kept in memory.\n\n**Evidence**: `cacher/cacher.go` L72, L92 (`Config.EventsHistoryWindow`).\n\n### 4.3 Admission Webhook Latency\n\n**Problem**: A slow mutating webhook blocks all creates/updates.\n\n**Mitigation**:\n- Webhooks run in parallel with 5s timeout (configurable)\n- Each webhook failure is logged; can be set to \"Fail Open\" (allow on error) or \"Fail Closed\" (deny)\n- Metrics track webhook latency\n\n**Risk**: Misconfigured webhook can cause cascading API server failures.\n\n**Evidence**: `admission/plugin/webhook/` plugins; metrics in `admission/metrics/metrics.go`.\n\n### 4.4 Resource Quota Enforcement\n\n**Design**: ResourceQuota controller watches namespace quotas and rejects creates that exceed limits.\n\n**Quirk**: Quota is **asynchronous**. A client might create 100 Pods rapidly, and the 101st gets quota rejection. In practice, short request bursts can exceed the quota.\n\n**Evidence**: `admission/plugin/resourcequota/` implements quota enforcement.\n\n### 4.5 Etcd Backend Dependency\n\n**Single point of failure**: If etcd is down, API server cannot persist writes.\n\n**Mitigation**:\n- Etcd is typically deployed as a 3-5 node cluster with raft consensus\n- API server reads/writes to any etcd node; raft handles replication\n\n**Limit**: API server **does not** handle etcd network partitions gracefully. If the etcd leader is unreachable, writes stall.\n\n**Evidence**: `storage/etcd3/store.go` uses `etcd/client/v3` directly.\n\n### 4.6 Request Timeouts and Deadlines\n\n**Design**:\n- Non-long-running requests (list, get, create, patch) have a strict deadline (default 30s)\n- Long-running requests (watch, exec, proxy) have no automatic timeout\n- Deadline is enforced via context timeout, not HTTP timeout\n\n**Implication**: If a storage operation takes >30s, the request is cancelled even if the operation completes (data races possible).\n\n**Evidence**: `server/config.go` L1067-1070 (`WithTimeoutForNonLongRunningRequests`, `WithRequestDeadline`).\n\n### 4.7 Discovery Cache Staleness\n\n**Trade-off**: Discovery endpoints (`/apis`, `/openapi/v3`) are cached. A new CRD might not appear in discovery for up to 30s.\n\n**Impact**: kubectl and client libraries may not immediately see new resources.\n\n**Evidence**: `endpoints/discovery/aggregated/` uses a caching layer; see `discovery.go` for cache TTL.\n\n---\n\n## 5. Extension Points and Customization Hooks\n\n### 5.1 Admission Control Plugins\n\n**Purpose**: Enforce policy on all API operations.\n\n**Interface** (see `admission/interfaces.go` L122-144):\n```go\ntype Interface interface {\n    Handles(operation Operation) bool\n}\n\ntype MutationInterface interface {\n    Interface\n    Admit(ctx context.Context, a Attributes, o ObjectInterfaces) error\n}\n\ntype ValidationInterface interface {\n    Interface\n    Validate(ctx context.Context, a Attributes, o ObjectInterfaces) error\n}\n```\n\n**How to extend**:\n1. Implement `Interface`, `MutationInterface`, or `ValidationInterface`\n2. Register in the admission plugin registry (see `admission/plugins.go`)\n3. Enable via `--admission-control` flag on `kube-apiserver`\n4. Access to full object; can read labels, annotations, ownership, etc.\n\n**Built-in examples**:\n- **PodSecurityPolicy**: Restrict security contexts (deprecated; use PodSecurityStandards)\n- **ResourceQuota**: Track and limit per-namespace resource consumption\n- **ServiceAccount**: Mutate pod specs to add service account tokens\n- **ValidatingAdmissionPolicy**: CEL-based validation rules\n\n**Risk**: Misconfigured admission plugin can break cluster (e.g., deny all creates). Use dry-run mode to test.\n\n### 5.2 REST Storage Strategy\n\n**Purpose**: Customize business logic for a resource type.\n\n**Interface** (see `registry/rest/rest.go` and `endpoints/handlers/`):\n```go\ntype Storage interface {\n    New() runtime.Object           // Return empty object\n    Destroy()                      // Clean up on shutdown\n}\n\ntype Creater interface {\n    Storage\n    Create(ctx, obj, createValidation, options) (runtime.Object, error)\n}\n\ntype Updater interface {\n    New() runtime.Object\n    Update(ctx, name, objInfo, createValidation, updateValidation, forceAllowCreate, options) (runtime.Object, bool, error)\n}\n\n// Similar for Getter, Deleter, Lister, Watcher\n```\n\n**How to extend**:\n1. Implement the REST interfaces (Creater, Updater, Getter, etc.)\n2. Wrap with admission validation using `rest.AdmissionToValidateObjectFunc()`\n3. Register in the API group's `VersionedResourcesStorageMap`\n4. Full control over validation, transformation, and storage backend\n\n**Example**: CRD validation schema + custom validation rules via CEL.\n\n**Risk**: Custom storage can introduce performance bottlenecks (e.g., calling external APIs on every create).\n\n### 5.3 Custom Authenticators and Authorizers\n\n**Authenticator** (see `authentication/authenticator/interfaces.go`):\n```go\ntype Token interface {\n    AuthenticateToken(ctx context.Context, tokenString string) (*Response, bool, error)\n}\n```\n\n**Authorizer** (see `authorization/authorizer/interfaces.go`):\n```go\ntype Authorizer interface {\n    Authorize(ctx context.Context, a Attributes) (Decision, string, error)\n}\n```\n\n**How to extend**:\n1. Implement `Token` or `Authorizer` interfaces\n2. Register via command-line flags:\n   - `--authentication-token-webhook-config-file`\n   - `--authentication-token-webhook-cache-ttl`\n   - `--authorization-webhook-config-file`\n   - Custom implementations can be compiled into a custom kube-apiserver binary\n\n**Limitations**: Webhooks are always authenticated with mTLS. Custom implementations must be in-process (compiled into the binary).\n\n### 5.4 CustomResourceDefinitions (CRDs)\n\n**Purpose**: Define new resource types without modifying server code.\n\n**How it works** (see `ARCHITECTURE.md` L54-65):\n1. User creates a `CustomResourceDefinition` object\n2. API extensions server watches for CRDs and dynamically creates REST handlers\n3. Handlers validate objects against the CRD's OpenAPI schema\n4. Objects stored in etcd under the CRD's group/version/kind path\n\n**Advantages**: Zero downtime; no recompilation; users define schemas declaratively.\n\n**Limitations**:\n- Cannot implement custom business logic (e.g., webhook validation is separate)\n- No built-in persistence transformation layer\n- Watch cache applies globally; cannot customize per-CRD\n\n### 5.5 APIServices (Aggregated API Servers)\n\n**Purpose**: Route requests to external API servers.\n\n**How it works** (see `ARCHITECTURE.md` L33-45):\n1. User creates an `APIService` object claiming a group/version (e.g., `example.com/v1`)\n2. Aggregator server watches APIServices and proxies matching requests\n3. Requests are forwarded to a backing `Service` (resolved via `ServiceResolver`)\n4. Responses are passed through transparently\n\n**Advantages**: Full control; can implement arbitrary logic; runs in separate process.\n\n**Limitations**:\n- Requires separate deployment\n- Must handle all requests (no fallback to extensions server)\n- Watch proxying not supported\n\n**Risk**: External API server crash or slowness affects the whole cluster.\n\n### 5.6 Field Manager and Server-Side Apply\n\n**Purpose**: Track which \"manager\" (user, controller) owns each field.\n\n**Interface** (see `endpoints/handlers/fieldmanager/`):\n```go\ntype FieldManager interface {\n    UpdateNoErrors(liveObj, newObj runtime.Object, manager string) runtime.Object\n}\n```\n\n**How to extend**:\n- Implement the FieldManager interface to customize merge semantics\n- Called from handlers during create/update/patch operations\n\n**Customization**: By default, uses `sigs.k8s.io/structured-merge-diff` library. Custom implementations can use different strategies.\n\n---\n\n## 6. Operational Implications: Performance, Reliability, and Maintainability\n\n### 6.1 Performance Characteristics\n\n**Request latency drivers**:\n1. **Serialization** (~1-5ms): JSON/protobuf encode/decode of request/response\n2. **Admission webhooks** (10-100ms): External policy checks; worst case: timeout (5s)\n3. **Storage operations** (1-50ms): etcd write; dominated by raft consensus latency\n4. **Cacher lookups** (<1ms): Extremely fast; in-memory hash lookups\n\n**Optimization levers**:\n- **Batch API** (e.g., POST with multiple objects): Reduces connection overhead\n- **Cacher tuning**: `--watch-cache=true` (default) dramatically improves list/watch latency\n- **Etcd backend tuning**: SSD storage, network latency between nodes critical\n- **Admission webhooks**: Use timeout budgets; disable non-critical webhooks\n- **Request filtering**: Use label/field selectors in LIST requests to reduce memory/CPU\n\n**Metrics to watch**:\n- `apiserver_request_duration_seconds`: Request latency histogram per verb/resource\n- `apiserver_current_inflight_requests`: In-flight request count\n- `apiserver_admission_webhook_admission_duration_seconds`: Webhook latency\n- `etcd_server_slow_apply_total`: Etcd operations taking >100ms\n\n### 6.2 Reliability and Failure Recovery\n\n**Failure modes and recovery**:\n\n| Failure | Symptom | Recovery |\n|---------|---------|----------|\n| Etcd down | All writes rejected; reads from cache still work | Restart etcd; API server will reconnect automatically |\n| Etcd leader election | Writes briefly stall (~1s) | Automatic; raft consensus handles |\n| Admission webhook crashes | Requests hang or rejected (if fail-closed) | Disable webhook; restart; check webhook logs |\n| API server memory leak | OOM killer terminates server | Restart; investigate cacher memory usage or leaked goroutines |\n| High CPU (watch cache churn) | Slow LIST/WATCH responses | Reduce number of resources; use namespaces for isolation |\n\n**High availability setup**:\n- Run 3+ API server replicas behind a load balancer\n- Each replica connects to the same etcd cluster\n- Requests are load-balanced (no session affinity needed)\n- Leader election not required (stateless design)\n\n### 6.3 Maintainability and Operational Burden\n\n**Key operational considerations**:\n\n1. **Configuration complexity**: `kube-apiserver` accepts 100+ flags (authentication, authorization, admission, encryption, audit, etc.). Misconfigurations are common.\n\n2. **Admission webhook management**: Over time, webhooks accumulate. Each adds latency and failure risk. Audit and clean up unused webhooks.\n\n3. **Audit logging overhead**: Audit log volume can be 1GB+/hour in large clusters. Consider log filtering and log rotation carefully.\n\n4. **Encryption key rotation**: Encryption keys are persisted in etcd. Rotation requires reading all data, re-encrypting, and writing back. Plan for downtime.\n\n5. **API deprecation**: Older API versions must be supported for backward compatibility. Removing versions requires careful planning and communication.\n\n6. **Custom resources proliferation**: Every CRD adds watch cache memory overhead. Monitor CRD count and consider limits.\n\n**Operational best practices**:\n- Monitor apiserver metrics continuously (prometheus + alerting)\n- Use dry-run mode for admission policies before production deployment\n- Test failover scenarios (etcd node failures, API server restarts)\n- Document custom admission policies and their business impact\n- Regularly review and prune unused resources (old API versions, webhooks, etc.)\n- Use RBAC to audit who is modifying API server configuration\n\n### 6.4 Debugging and Troubleshooting\n\n**Available debug tools**:\n\n| Tool | Purpose | Access |\n|------|---------|--------|\n| `--v=4` | Klog verbosity level | Command-line flag |\n| `--audit-policy-file` | Per-request audit logging | File-based configuration |\n| `/metrics` | Prometheus metrics | HTTP endpoint (usually secured) |\n| `pprof` debug socket | CPU/memory profiling | Unix socket at `--debug-socket-path` |\n| `/debug/flags` | View/modify feature gates at runtime | Unprotected HTTP endpoint |\n| `/openapi/v3` | OpenAPI schema for all resources | HTTP endpoint |\n| `--enable-priority-and-fairness-logging` | Track request scheduling | Command-line flag |\n\n**Common debugging scenarios**:\n\n1. **Request stuck/slow**:\n   - Check `apiserver_current_inflight_requests` metric\n   - Look for slow admission webhooks in logs (`--v=4`)\n   - Check etcd latency: `etcd_server_slow_apply_total`\n\n2. **Watch stream disconnected**:\n   - Check if cacher's watch history window expired\n   - Verify etcd health (leadership, storage, network)\n   - Increase `--watch-cache-sizes` if high-frequency resource\n\n3. **Admission webhook rejecting valid requests**:\n   - Test webhook with dry-run first\n   - Check webhook server logs (external to API server)\n   - Temporarily disable webhook to isolate issue\n\n---\n\n## 7. Important Files and Directory Map\n\n| Path | Purpose |\n|------|---------|\n| `pkg/server/config.go` | Config struct, handler chain builder (`DefaultBuildHandlerChain`) |\n| `pkg/server/genericapiserver.go` | Main GenericAPIServer runtime; API group registration |\n| `pkg/endpoints/installer.go` | REST route installation; URL to handler mapping |\n| `pkg/endpoints/handlers/create.go` | POST request handling; admission invocation flow |\n| `pkg/endpoints/handlers/get.go` | GET request handling; response serialization |\n| `pkg/endpoints/handlers/watch.go` | Watch request handling; stream upgrades |\n| `pkg/admission/interfaces.go` | Admission plugin interfaces (MutationInterface, ValidationInterface) |\n| `pkg/admission/plugins.go` | Built-in admission plugin registry |\n| `pkg/authentication/authenticator/interfaces.go` | Token authenticator interface |\n| `pkg/authorization/authorizer/interfaces.go` | Authorizer interface |\n| `pkg/storage/interfaces.go` | Storage.Interface and options |\n| `pkg/storage/cacher/cacher.go` | In-memory watch cache implementation |\n| `pkg/storage/etcd3/store.go` | Etcd v3 backend storage implementation |\n| `pkg/registry/generic/registry/store.go` | genericregistry.Store; wraps storage with REST adapter |\n| `pkg/util/flowcontrol/` | Priority and Fairness (request queue management) |\n| `pkg/audit/` | Audit logging policy and event recording |\n\n---\n\n## 8. Design Tradeoffs Summary\n\n| Tradeoff | Choice | Rationale |\n|----------|--------|-----------|\n| **Optimistic concurrency** | No server-side locking | Scales better; simpler design; requires client retry logic |\n| **Watch cache** | In-memory buffer of events | Dramatically reduces etcd load; adds memory overhead; can expire if events old |\n| **Admission before deserialization** | No, after auth/authz | Saves CPU parsing untrusted requests; makes admission access to full object |\n| **Request timeouts** | Context-based, not HTTP | Prevents slow clients from blocking; allows operation to complete async |\n| **Plugin architecture** | Webhooks for extensibility | Dynamic (no recompile); adds latency and failure risk; not strongly typed |\n| **Storage backends** | Pluggable (etcd, etc.) | Allows experimentation; in practice, only etcd is production-ready |\n| **API versioning** | Multiple in-flight versions | Backward compat; complicates conversion logic; adds server memory/CPU |\n\n---\n\n## 9. Conclusion\n\nThe Kubernetes API server library is a sophisticated, production-grade framework for building REST APIs with strong consistency guarantees, security, policy enforcement, and scalability. Its architecture balances simplicity (stateless servers, no leases) with performance (watch caching) and flexibility (pluggable authentication, authorization, admission).\n\n**Key insights**:\n\n1. **Request isolation**: Each request is independent; no shared state between servers.\n2. **Admission is the main hook**: Most customization happens via admission webhooks or plugins.\n3. **Cacher is critical**: Watch cache is the secret weapon that makes Kubernetes scalable.\n4. **Etcd is the source of truth**: All durability guarantees depend on etcd correctness.\n5. **Operational complexity**: Many knobs and plugins; requires careful tuning and monitoring.\n\nFor teams building Kubernetes-like systems or extensions, the library is well-designed for reuse. The main challenges are understanding the complex request flow, debugging distributed issues, and managing the operational burden of production deployments.\n"},"caller":{"type":"direct"}}],"stop_reason":null,"stop_sequence":null,"usage":{"input_tokens":7,"cache_creation_input_tokens":1793,"cache_read_input_tokens":81602,"cache_creation":{"ephemeral_5m_input_tokens":0,"ephemeral_1h_input_tokens":1793},"output_tokens":1,"service_tier":"standard","inference_geo":"not_available"},"context_management":null},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"96b17583-ccba-4b45-a8c8-98253d4490f6","uuid":"6679641a-ca01-4315-8c8c-4469fb8eb2b9"}
